Practice Ladder - Installation & ConfigurationGuided Practice

Run git config --list --show-origin and identify where your username, email, and default branch are configured.

bash 
# Download from https://git-scm.com/downloads
# Verify installation:
git --version   # git version 2.x.x

# Configure your identity (do this once)
git config --global user.name "Your Name"
git config --global user.email "you@example.com"

# Set VS Code as default editor
git config --global core.editor "code --wait"

# Verify config
git config --list

Essential Commands

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Essential commands exist to move work through Git’s workflow: inspect, stage, commit, review, and publish.

What if it did not exist?

Without command fluency, developers guess. Guessing with Git leads to accidental commits, missing files, and fear of changing history.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use git status constantly. It tells you what Git sees and often tells you the next command.

Intermediate

Pair commands: diff before add, diff --staged before commit, log after commit.

Advanced

Use partial staging and focused commits to separate unrelated changes.

Mastery

Every command should answer a question: what changed, what is staged, where am I, what history exists, or what needs publishing?

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Essential commands exist to move work through Git’s workflow: inspect, stage, commit, review, and publish. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Essential Commands, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
git status
git diff
git add index.html
git commit -m "docs: expand git lesson"

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without command fluency, developers guess. Guessing with Git leads to accidental commits, missing files, and fear of changing history. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
# guessing commands
git add .
git commit -m "stuff"
# unrelated debug files may be included

Learning Ladder in Detail

Beginner Level - Build the First Mental Model

Use git status constantly. It tells you what Git sees and often tells you the next command. At this stage, your job is not speed. Your job is to say what each line does in plain language. Use tiny inputs and tiny outputs. If you cannot predict the result before running it, make the example smaller.

beginner example
git status

Beginner practice: copy the example exactly, run it, then change one name and one value. Explain what changed and what stayed the same.

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Pair commands: diff before add, diff --staged before commit, log after commit. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
git diff --staged

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Use partial staging and focused commits to separate unrelated changes. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
git add -p

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Every command should answer a question: what changed, what is staged, where am I, what history exists, or what needs publishing? Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
git log --oneline --stat -5

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - Essential CommandsGuided Practice

Modify two files, stage only one, commit it, then stage and commit the second. Verify with git log --stat.

The commands below are not random vocabulary. They move changes between four places: your working tree, the staging area, your local repository, and GitHub. If you know where your change currently lives, you can choose the right command instead of guessing.

Command Flow Example: From Edit to Published Change

bash
# 1. See what changed in the working tree
git status

# 2. Review the exact edits before staging
git diff

# 3. Stage only the file that belongs in this commit
git add src/App.jsx

# 4. Review the staged change
git diff --staged

# 5. Save a meaningful checkpoint
git commit -m "feat: add lesson progress card"

# 6. Publish local commits to GitHub
git push origin main

Starting a Repository

bash 
# Initialize a new repo in current folder
git init

# Or clone an existing remote repo
git clone https://github.com/user/repo.git

# Check the status of your working directory
git status

The Three-Stage Model

Understanding Git's three areas is crucial:

  1. Working Directory — your files as they are on disk right now
  2. Staging Area (Index) — files you've marked to be in the next commit
  3. Repository — the permanent history of committed snapshots
bash 
# Stage a specific file
git add index.html

# Stage ALL changed files
git add .

# Commit staged changes with a message
git commit -m "feat: add homepage layout"

# Stage and commit in one step (only for tracked files)
git commit -am "fix: correct typo in nav"

# View commit history
git log --oneline --graph --decorate

Conventional Commits

A widely-used standard for commit messages: type: description

  • feat: — new feature
  • fix: — bug fix
  • docs: — documentation only
  • style: — formatting, no logic change
  • refactor: — code change that isn't a fix or feature
  • test: — adding tests
  • chore: — build process or auxiliary tool changes

Branching & Merging

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Branching exists so work can happen in parallel. Merging exists to bring completed work back together.

What if it did not exist?

Without branches, experiments happen directly on the main line and unfinished work blocks stable progress.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

A branch is a label for a line of work. Create one for a feature, fix, or experiment.

Intermediate

Keep branches focused and short-lived so merges stay simple.

Advanced

Resolve conflicts by understanding both intentions, not by automatically choosing one side.

Mastery

Use branch strategy to support review, release, and collaboration habits for the team.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Branching exists so work can happen in parallel. Merging exists to bring completed work back together. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Branching & Merging, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
git switch -c feature/search
# build safely away from main
git switch main
git merge feature/search

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without branches, experiments happen directly on the main line and unfinished work blocks stable progress. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
# all experiments happen on main
# broken work blocks stable releases

Learning Ladder in Detail

Beginner Level - Build the First Mental Model

A branch is a label for a line of work. Create one for a feature, fix, or experiment. At this stage, your job is not speed. Your job is to say what each line does in plain language. Use tiny inputs and tiny outputs. If you cannot predict the result before running it, make the example smaller.

beginner example
git branch
git switch -c my-first-branch

Beginner practice: copy the example exactly, run it, then change one name and one value. Explain what changed and what stayed the same.

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Keep branches focused and short-lived so merges stay simple. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
git merge feature/add-about

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Resolve conflicts by understanding both intentions, not by automatically choosing one side. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. advanced example

# edit conflicted files
git add .
git commit

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Use branch strategy to support review, release, and collaboration habits for the team. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
short-lived branches + focused pull requests + clean merge history

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - Branching & MergingGuided Practice

Create two branches that edit the same heading differently. Merge one, then merge the other and resolve the conflict manually.

A branch is a movable label pointing at a commit. Beginners use branches to avoid breaking main. Professionals use branches to organize reviewable units of work. Mastery means keeping branches short-lived and resolving conflicts by understanding intent, not by blindly choosing one side.

Branching Example: Small Feature, Clean Merge

bash
git switch -c feature/progress-meter
# edit files, test the behavior
git add index.html
git commit -m "feat: add progress meter"
git switch main
git merge feature/progress-meter
git branch -d feature/progress-meter
Conflict habit
When a conflict happens, first ask: "What was each branch trying to accomplish?" Then edit the file into the final intended version and commit that resolution.
bash 
# List all branches (* = current)
git branch

# Create and switch to a new branch
git checkout -b feature/add-login

# Modern equivalent (Git 2.23+)
git switch -c feature/add-login

# Switch back to main
git switch main

# Merge feature branch into main
git merge feature/add-login

# Delete a merged branch
git branch -d feature/add-login

Working with GitHub

What if it did not exist?

Without a remote host, your Git history stays on one machine and collaboration or deployment becomes manual.

Push your local commits to GitHub so the project has a remote copy.

Intermediate

Advanced

Use issues, branches, protected main, and Actions to create a reliable workflow.

Mastery

# Link a local repo to GitHub remote

# Push commits to GitHub (first time)

# Subsequent pushes

Exercise 1.1 — Init & First Commit Day 1 · Exercise 1

Create a new folder called my-portfolio, initialize a Git repository, create an index.html with a basic heading, and make your first commit with the message feat: initial portfolio setup.

bash
mkdir my-portfolio
cd my-portfolio
git init
# Create index.html
echo '<!DOCTYPE html><html><body><h1>My Portfolio</h1></body></html>' > index.html
git add index.html
git commit -m "feat: initial portfolio setup"
git log --oneline
Exercise 1.2 — Feature Branch Workflow Day 1 · Exercise 2

From your my-portfolio repo: create a branch called feature/add-about, add an about.html page, commit it, then merge it back to main and delete the feature branch.

bash
git switch -c feature/add-about
echo '<!DOCTYPE html><html><body><h1>About Me</h1></body></html>' > about.html
git add about.html
git commit -m "feat: add about page"
git switch main
git merge feature/add-about
git branch -d feature/add-about
git log --oneline --graph
Exercise 1.3 — Resolving a Merge Conflict Day 1 · Exercise 3

Create two branches from main — both modify the same line of index.html. Merge one, then merge the other and manually resolve the conflict that appears.

bash
# Create branch-a and change the title
git switch -c branch-a
# Edit index.html: change <h1> to "Portfolio - Version A"
git commit -am "chore: version A title"

# Create branch-b from main and change the title differently
git switch main
git switch -c branch-b
# Edit index.html: change <h1> to "Portfolio - Version B"
git commit -am "chore: version B title"

# Merge branch-a into main first
git switch main
git merge branch-a

# Now merge branch-b — CONFLICT!
git merge branch-b
# Open index.html, look for conflict markers:
# <<<<<<< HEAD ... ======= ... >>>>>>>
# Choose the version you want, remove markers
git add index.html
git commit -m "merge: resolve title conflict"
Day 1 — Deep Dive

Git Internals

Understanding what Git actually stores under the hood makes you a better developer.

Git Objects

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Git objects exist as the storage model underneath commits. They explain how Git saves content, directories, and history reliably.

What if it did not exist?

Without this model, Git feels mysterious and recovery tools feel dangerous.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Know the four names: blob stores file content, tree stores directory structure, commit stores a snapshot plus metadata, tag names a release.

Intermediate

Use git cat-file and git ls-tree to inspect what a commit points to.

Advanced

Understand that Git identifies content by hash, so identical content can be recognized and history can be verified.

Mastery

Use internals knowledge to debug unusual repository problems without panic.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Git objects exist as the storage model underneath commits. They explain how Git saves content, directories, and history reliably. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Git Objects, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

git log --oneline

Practice Ladder - Git ObjectsGuided Practice

Create a commit, inspect HEAD, inspect its tree, and find the blob for one file.

Git internals are useful because they remove fear. A commit is not a vague save point; it is an object that points to a tree, which points to file blobs. This is why Git can compare versions quickly and recover committed work reliably.

Inspect the Object Chain

bash
git rev-parse HEAD

git cat-file -p HEAD

git cat-file -p HEAD^{tree}

git ls-tree -r --name-only HEAD

Beginner reading: HEAD means "the commit I am on." Mastery reading: Git can rebuild your project because each commit names a complete tree of file contents.

Every piece of data in Git is stored as one of four object types in the .git/objects/ directory. Git is fundamentally a content-addressable filesystem.

Blob

Stores file contents. No filename — just the raw bytes. Two identical files share one blob.

Tree

Stores a directory listing: maps file names to blob SHAs. Like an inode directory entry.

Commit

Points to a tree (root snapshot), parent commit(s), author, timestamp, and message.

Tag

An annotated pointer to a commit with a message — used to mark releases like v1.0.0.

bash — Explore Git Objects
# See all objects in your repo
find .git/objects -type f

# Read any object by its SHA
git cat-file -t <sha>  # type: blob/tree/commit/tag
git cat-file -p <sha>  # pretty-print contents

# See the tree of the latest commit
git cat-file -p HEAD^{tree}

# Hash a file without storing it
git hash-object myfile.txt

The Staging Area (Index)

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

The staging area exists so the next commit can be deliberately assembled instead of blindly saving every file.

What if it did not exist?

Without staging, every commit would include all current changes, even unrelated experiments or debug edits.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Think of staging as a shopping basket for your next commit.

Intermediate

Stage related changes together and leave unrelated work unstaged.

Advanced

Use patch staging to split changes inside the same file.

Mastery

Craft commits that tell one clear story each, making review and rollback easier.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

The staging area exists so the next commit can be deliberately assembled instead of blindly saving every file. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with The Staging Area (Index), look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
// The Staging Area (Index)
const example = "small practical use"

Practice Ladder - The Staging Area (Index)Guided Practice

Make three changes in one file and use patch staging to commit only one of them.

The index (.git/index) is a binary file that acts as the staging area. It tracks the state of each file — its SHA, permissions, and modification time. git add updates the index; git commit creates a tree from it.

bash
# View the staging area
git ls-files --stage

# Compare working dir vs staging
git diff

# Compare staging vs last commit
git diff --staged

# Partially stage a file (interactive hunks)
git add -p myfile.js

Reflog — Your Safety Net

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Reflog exists to record where your branch tips and HEAD have recently been. It is Git’s local safety trail.

What if it did not exist?

Without reflog, recovering from accidental resets, rebases, or deleted branches would be much harder.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Remember: if you recently had a commit checked out, reflog may know where it was.

Intermediate

Use git reflog to find lost commit hashes and create a rescue branch.

Advanced

Understand reflog is local and temporary, so it is not a replacement for pushing important work.

Mastery

Recover confidently by inspecting first, branching second, and resetting only when you know the target.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Reflog exists to record where your branch tips and HEAD have recently been. It is Git’s local safety trail. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

git diff && git add .

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Understand reflog is local and temporary, so it is not a replacement for pushing important work. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
git log --graph --oneline

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Recover confidently by inspecting first, branching second, and resetting only when you know the target. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
# focused branch, focused commit, reviewed merge

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - Reflog — Your Safety NetGuided Practice

Create a commit, reset away from it, find it in reflog, and recover it with a new branch.

The reflog records every time a branch tip moves. Even after git reset --hard or deleting a branch, you can recover commits via reflog for up to 90 days.

bash
# View all recorded HEAD movements
git reflog

# Recover a lost commit
git reflog  # find the SHA
git switch -c rescue-branch <sha>

# Undo a git reset --hard (within 90 days)
git reflog  # find SHA before the reset
git reset --hard <sha>

Git Config Levels

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Config levels exist so Git can have system-wide, user-wide, and project-specific settings.

What if it did not exist?

Without levels, every repository would need repeated setup or global settings would be impossible to override.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use global config for your name, email, and default editor.

Intermediate

Use local config when one repository needs different behavior.

Advanced

Inspect origins to solve confusing settings with --show-origin.

Mastery

Document team-specific config assumptions so onboarding is predictable.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Config levels exist so Git can have system-wide, user-wide, and project-specific settings. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Git Config Levels, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
# Git Config Levels
git status
git log --oneline

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without levels, every repository would need repeated setup or global settings would be impossible to override. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
# no clear history, no safe recovery, no collaboration trail

Practice Ladder - Git Config LevelsGuided Practice

Set a local config value in a test repo and compare it with your global config.

bash
# System level (all users, all repos)
git config --system core.autocrlf true

# Global level (current user, all repos)
git config --global user.name "Alice"
git config --global alias.lg "log --oneline --graph --all"

# Local level (this repo only) — highest priority
git config --local core.ignorecase false

# View merged config
git config --list --show-origin

# Use your alias
git lg
Exercise — Explore .git DirectoryDay 1B · Exercise

In any Git repo: create a file, stage it, and commit. Then use git cat-file to read the blob, tree, and commit objects manually. Trace the full chain from commit SHA → tree SHA → blob SHA → file contents.

bash
echo "Hello Git" > hello.txt
git add hello.txt
git commit -m "feat: add hello.txt"

# Get the commit SHA
git log --oneline -1   # e.g. a1b2c3d

# Read the commit object
git cat-file -p a1b2c3d
# Output: tree <tree-sha>, parent, author, message

# Read the tree object
git cat-file -p <tree-sha>
# Output: blob <blob-sha> hello.txt

# Read the blob
git cat-file -p <blob-sha>
# Output: Hello Git
Day 2

Node, npm & Vite Setup

Setting up the modern JavaScript development environment.

Node.js & npm

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Node exists to run JavaScript outside the browser. npm exists to install packages and run project scripts. Modern React tooling depends on both.

What if it did not exist?

Without Node/npm, you could write plain browser JavaScript, but you would lose the modern package ecosystem, build tools, dev servers, and testing workflow.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Install Node LTS, check versions, and understand that npm install reads your package files.

Intermediate

Know the difference between dependencies, dev dependencies, scripts, and the lockfile.

Advanced

Diagnose install problems by reading error messages and checking Node version compatibility.

Mastery

Create reproducible project setup that works for you, your team, and CI.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Node exists to run JavaScript outside the browser. npm exists to install packages and run project scripts. Modern React tooling depends on both. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Node.js & npm, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
node --version
npm install
npm run dev

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without Node/npm, you could write plain browser JavaScript, but you would lose the modern package ecosystem, build tools, dev servers, and testing workflow. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - Node.js & npmGuided Practice

Create a Vite app, run dev, build it, preview it, and explain which command belongs to Node, npm, and Vite.

Node runs JavaScript outside the browser. npm downloads packages and runs project scripts. Vite uses both to give you a fast React development server and a production build. Keeping those responsibilities separate makes setup problems much easier to debug.

Beginner-to-Professional Setup Check

bash
node --version
npm --version
npm create vite@latest my-react-app -- --template react
cd my-react-app
npm install
npm run dev

If one step fails, identify which tool failed. A Node version error is different from an npm install error, and both are different from a React runtime error in the browser.

Node.js is a JavaScript runtime that lets you run JS outside the browser. npm (Node Package Manager) comes bundled with Node and manages third-party libraries.

bash
# Install from https://nodejs.org (LTS version)
node --version   # v20.x.x
npm --version    # 10.x.x

# Install pnpm (faster alternative, optional)
npm install -g pnpm

Understanding package.json

The package.json file is the heart of every Node project — it describes the project, its scripts, and its dependencies.

json
{
  "name": "my-react-app",
  "version": "1.0.0",
  "scripts": {
    "dev": "vite",           // npm run dev → starts dev server
    "build": "vite build",  // npm run build → production build
    "preview": "vite preview"
  },
  "dependencies": {         // needed in production
    "react": "^18.2.0",
    "react-dom": "^18.2.0"
  },
  "devDependencies": {     // only needed during development
    "vite": "^5.0.0",
    "@vitejs/plugin-react": "^4.0.0"
  }
}

Creating a Vite + React Project

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Vite exists to give React projects a fast development server, modern module handling, and optimized production builds.

What if it did not exist?

Without a tool like Vite, you would manually wire bundling, JSX transformation, module loading, and development refresh behavior.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use the template command and learn the folder structure before changing many files.

Intermediate

Understand src/main.jsx as the app entry and src/App.jsx as the first component.

Advanced

Configure plugins, aliases, environment variables, and build output only when the project needs them.

Mastery

Keep tooling boring and reliable. The tool should support product work, not become the product.

Practice Ladder - Creating a Vite + React ProjectGuided Practice

Create a Vite project, add one component, build it, and inspect the generated dist folder.

Vite is a blazing-fast build tool that replaces Create React App. It starts instantly and has hot module replacement (HMR).

bash
# Create a new Vite + React project
npm create vite@latest my-react-app -- --template react

# Navigate and install dependencies
cd my-react-app
npm install

# Start development server
npm run dev
# → Local: http://localhost:5173

Vite Project Structure

tree
my-react-app/
├── public/           ← static assets (favicon etc)
├── src/
│   ├── assets/       ← images, fonts
│   ├── App.jsx       ← root component
│   ├── App.css
│   ├── main.jsx      ← entry point
│   └── index.css
├── index.html        ← HTML template
├── vite.config.js
└── package.json

Essential npm Commands

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

npm scripts exist to standardize repeated project tasks like development, testing, linting, building, and previewing.

What if it did not exist?

Without scripts, every developer remembers different commands and CI cannot reliably repeat local checks.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Run scripts with npm run name. Start with dev, build, and preview.

Intermediate

Add scripts for linting and tests so quality checks become one command.

Advanced

Compose scripts carefully so failures stop the pipeline and output remains readable.

Mastery

Design scripts as project contracts: a new developer should know exactly how to run and verify the app.

Practice Ladder - Essential npm CommandsGuided Practice

Add a check script that runs lint, tests, and build in order.

Every command in package.json is a repeatable project task. Beginners run scripts. Intermediate developers edit scripts. Experts design scripts so a teammate or CI server can reproduce the same checks without hidden manual steps.

Script Design Example

json
{
  "scripts": {
    "dev": "vite",
    "build": "vite build",
    "preview": "vite preview",
    "lint": "eslint .",
    "test": "vitest run",
    "check": "npm run lint && npm run test && npm run build"
  }
}
bash
# Install a package (saves to dependencies)
npm install react-router-dom

# Install a dev dependency
npm install --save-dev prettier

# Install globally (available everywhere)
npm install -g serve

# Remove a package
npm uninstall lodash

# List installed packages
npm list

# Update packages
npm update

# Check for outdated packages
npm outdated

ESLint & Prettier Setup

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

ESLint exists to catch code problems and enforce rules. Prettier exists to format code consistently so humans stop arguing about spacing.

What if it did not exist?

Without linting and formatting, style drift and simple mistakes pile up until reviews become noisy.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Let Prettier format automatically and read ESLint warnings as coaching.

Intermediate

Understand which rules prevent real bugs and which rules are team preferences.

Advanced

Tune config gradually instead of disabling rules whenever they complain.

Mastery

Use automated checks in CI so quality is consistent and not dependent on memory.

Code quality tools that catch bugs and enforce consistent style automatically.

bash
# Install Prettier

# Create .prettierrc
echo '{"semi":false,"singleQuote":true,"tabWidth":2}' > .prettierrc

# Format all files
npx prettier --write src/

Exercise 2.2 — Git + npm Project Day 2 · Exercise 2

Initialize git in your Vite app. Create a .gitignore that excludes node_modules and dist. Make an initial commit, then create and push to a new GitHub repo.

bash
git init
echo -e "node_modules/\ndist/\n.env" > .gitignore
git add .
git commit -m "feat: initial Vite React scaffold"
# Create repo on GitHub, then:
git remote add origin https://github.com/you/day2-app.git
git branch -M main
git push -u origin main
Day 2 — Deep Dive

npm Ecosystem Deep Dive

package.json anatomy, semantic versioning, scripts, workspaces, and the lock file explained.

Semantic Versioning (SemVer)

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

SemVer exists to communicate what kind of change a package release contains: breaking change, new feature, or bug fix.

What if it did not exist?

Without version meaning, updating packages would be pure guesswork and every install could change behavior unpredictably.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Read versions as major.minor.patch.

Intermediate

Understand ranges like caret and tilde, and why lockfiles still matter.

Advanced

Audit updates before merging major version changes.

Mastery

Plan dependency updates as maintenance work with testing, changelog review, and rollback awareness.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

SemVer exists to communicate what kind of change a package release contains: breaking change, new feature, or bug fix. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Semantic Versioning (SemVer), look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
# Semantic Versioning (SemVer)
npm run dev
npm run build

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - Semantic Versioning (SemVer)Guided Practice

Pick one dependency, inspect its current version, check what a patch/minor/major update would mean, and explain the risk.

SemVer is a promise about change. Read MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH as: breaking changes, new compatible features, bug fixes. This matters because dependency updates can change your app even when your own code did not change.

RangeWhat it allowsHow to think about it
1.2.3Only 1.2.3Maximum reproducibility
^1.2.31.x updates below 2.0.0Common app default
~1.2.3Patch updates below 1.3.0Smaller update window

Every npm package version follows the pattern MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH — for example 18.2.0.

MAJOR (18)

Breaking changes. Your code may need updates to work with a new major version.

MINOR (2)

New features added in a backwards-compatible way. Safe to upgrade.

PATCH (0)

Bug fixes only. Always safe to upgrade.

json — Version Range Symbols
{
  "react": "18.2.0",     // exact version — never update automatically
  "react": "^18.2.0",    // caret: >=18.2.0 <19.0.0 (same major)
  "react": "~18.2.0",    // tilde: >=18.2.0 <18.3.0 (same minor)
  "react": "*",           // any version — dangerous, never use
  "react": ">=18.0.0",    // greater than or equal
  "react": "18.x"         // any patch/minor of major 18
}

The package-lock.json File

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

The lockfile exists to record the exact dependency tree that was installed.

What if it did not exist?

Without it, two machines can install slightly different dependency versions from the same package.json.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Commit the lockfile for applications.

Intermediate

Use npm ci in CI for clean reproducible installs.

Advanced

Review lockfile changes when dependencies update unexpectedly.

Mastery

Treat dependency reproducibility as part of application reliability.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

The lockfile exists to record the exact dependency tree that was installed. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with The package-lock.json File, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
# The package-lock.json File
npm run dev
npm run build

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without it, two machines can install slightly different dependency versions from the same package.json. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
# manual setup differs on every machine

Learning Ladder in Detail

Beginner Level - Build the First Mental Model

Commit the lockfile for applications. At this stage, your job is not speed. Your job is to say what each line does in plain language. Use tiny inputs and tiny outputs. If you cannot predict the result before running it, make the example smaller.

beginner example
npm run dev

Beginner practice: copy the example exactly, run it, then change one name and one value. Explain what changed and what stayed the same.

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Use npm ci in CI for clean reproducible installs. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
npm run build

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Review lockfile changes when dependencies update unexpectedly. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
npm ci

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Treat dependency reproducibility as part of application reliability. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
npm run check

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - The package-lock.json FileGuided Practice

Delete node_modules, run npm ci, and compare that to npm install conceptually.

While package.json specifies version ranges, package-lock.json records the exact versions that were installed. This guarantees every team member and every CI server installs identical packages.

⚠️ Always Commit package-lock.json
Never add package-lock.json to .gitignore. It is a critical file for reproducible builds. Always use npm ci (not npm install) in CI/CD pipelines — it installs exactly what the lock file says.
bash
# Install exactly what package-lock.json says (use in CI)
npm ci

# Update a specific package to latest within its range
npm update react

# Update to latest regardless of range (careful!)
npm install react@latest

# Check which packages have newer versions available
npm outdated

# See why a package is installed (dependency chain)
npm why lodash

# Audit for known vulnerabilities
npm audit
npm audit fix        # auto-fix safe updates
npm audit fix --force # force all fixes (may break things)

Advanced

Compose scripts so local checks match CI checks.

Practice Ladder - Writing Useful npm ScriptsGuided Practice

Add scripts for format, lint, and check, then explain what each one guarantees.

The "scripts" field in package.json lets you define commands runnable with npm run. Scripts can reference other scripts, run in parallel, and chain commands.

json — package.json scripts
{
  "scripts": {
    "dev": "vite",
    "build": "vite build",
    "preview": "vite preview",

    // Lint and format
    "lint": "eslint src --ext .js,.jsx --report-unused-disable-directives",
    "lint:fix": "eslint src --ext .js,.jsx --fix",
    "format": "prettier --write src/",
    "format:check": "prettier --check src/",

    // Tests
    "test": "vitest",
    "test:run": "vitest run",
    "test:coverage": "vitest run --coverage",

    // Run multiple in sequence with &&
    "check": "npm run lint && npm run format:check && npm run test:run",

    // Pre/post hooks run automatically
    "prebuild": "npm run lint",   // runs before npm run build
    "postbuild": "echo Build complete!"
  }
}

npx — Run Without Installing

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

npx exists to run package-provided commands without manually installing them globally.

What if it did not exist?

Without it, developers would install many global tools that go stale or conflict across projects.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use npx when documentation asks you to run a one-time package command.

Intermediate

Prefer project scripts for repeated commands and npx for scaffolding or temporary tools.

Advanced

Know when npx downloads a package and when it uses a local binary.

Mastery

Avoid global tool drift by keeping project tooling inside the project whenever possible.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

npx exists to run package-provided commands without manually installing them globally. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Exercise 2B.1 — package.json MasteryDay 2B · Exercise 1

Create a package.json from scratch (using npm init -y) for a project called react-blog. Then: (a) install react and react-dom as dependencies, (b) install vite, @vitejs/plugin-react, and prettier as devDependencies, (c) add a format script that runs prettier on the src/ folder, (d) set the main field to "src/main.jsx".

bash
mkdir react-blog && cd react-blog
npm init -y
npm install react react-dom
npm install --save-dev vite @vitejs/plugin-react prettier
json — resulting package.json
{
  "name": "react-blog",
  "version": "1.0.0",
  "main": "src/main.jsx",
  "scripts": {
    "dev": "vite",
    "build": "vite build",
    "format": "prettier --write src/"
  },
  "dependencies": {
    "react": "^18.x.x",
    "react-dom": "^18.x.x"
  },
  "devDependencies": {
    "vite": "^5.x.x",
    "@vitejs/plugin-react": "^4.x.x",
    "prettier": "^3.x.x"
  }
}
Day 3

React Core — JSX & Components

The building blocks of every React application.

"React makes it painless to create interactive UIs."
React Documentation

What is JSX?

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

JSX exists to describe UI structure while staying inside JavaScript. It lets markup and the data that controls it live together.

What if it did not exist?

Without JSX, React code would use nested React.createElement calls that are harder for beginners and teams to read.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Think of JSX as HTML-like syntax with JavaScript expressions inside braces.

Intermediate

Move complicated logic above the return so JSX remains readable.

Advanced

Understand JSX compiles to JavaScript, so attributes and expressions follow JavaScript rules.

Mastery

Write JSX that communicates UI states clearly: loading, empty, error, and success.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

JSX exists to describe UI structure while staying inside JavaScript. It lets markup and the data that controls it live together. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with What is JSX?, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
const title = "React"
return <h1>{title}</h1>

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without JSX, React code would use nested React.createElement calls that are harder for beginners and teams to read. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
return React.createElement("h1", null, title)

Learning Ladder in Detail

Beginner Level - Build the First Mental Model

Think of JSX as HTML-like syntax with JavaScript expressions inside braces. At this stage, your job is not speed. Your job is to say what each line does in plain language. Use tiny inputs and tiny outputs. If you cannot predict the result before running it, make the example smaller.

beginner example
return <p>Hello {name}</p>

Beginner practice: copy the example exactly, run it, then change one name and one value. Explain what changed and what stayed the same.

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Move complicated logic above the return so JSX remains readable. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
const label = done ? "Complete" : "Continue"
return <button>{label}</button>

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Understand JSX compiles to JavaScript, so attributes and expressions follow JavaScript rules. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
if (loading) return <Spinner />
if (error) return <ErrorMessage />
return <LessonList lessons={lessons} />

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Write JSX that communicates UI states clearly: loading, empty, error, and success. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
clean JSX: prepared data above, clear states below, no tangled expressions

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - What is JSX?Guided Practice

Render a name, conditionally show a message, and map an array into a list of components.

JSX lets you describe UI with HTML-like syntax while still using JavaScript values. The beginner task is learning the syntax differences. The mastery task is deciding how much logic belongs in the JSX and how much should be prepared before the return.

JSX Progression Example

jsx
// Beginner: render a value
function Welcome({ name }) {
  return <h2>Hello, {name}</h2>
}

// Intermediate: derive a readable value before JSX
function LessonStatus({ completed, total }) {
  const label = completed === total ? 'Complete' : completed + ' of ' + total
  return <p>{label}</p>
}

// Mastery: handle edge states before normal UI
function LessonList({ lessons }) {
  if (lessons.length === 0) return <p>No lessons yet.</p>
  return lessons.map(lesson => <LessonCard key={lesson.id} lesson={lesson} />)
}

JSX is a syntax extension that looks like HTML but is actually JavaScript. It gets compiled to React.createElement() calls by Vite/Babel.

jsx
// JSX — what you write:
const element = <h1 className="title">Hello, World!</h1>

// What it compiles to:
const element = React.createElement(
  'h1',
  { className: 'title' },
  'Hello, World!'
)

JSX Rules

⚠️ Key JSX Differences from HTML
  • Use className instead of class
  • Use htmlFor instead of for
  • All tags must be closed: <img />, <br />
  • Must return a single root element — use <React.Fragment> or <></>
  • JavaScript expressions go inside { curly braces }
jsx
const user = { name: 'Alice', age: 28 }

function UserCard() {
  return (
    <>
      <h2>{user.name}</h2>
      <p>Age: {user.age}</p>
      <p>{user.age >= 18 ? 'Adult' : 'Minor'}</p>
      <img src={user.avatar} alt={user.name} />
    </>
  )
}

Function Components

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Function components exist to package UI into reusable pieces. They are the basic building blocks of modern React apps.

What if it did not exist?

Without components, every screen becomes one large file with repeated markup and tangled logic.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

A component is a function that returns UI.

Intermediate

Pass data through props and split components when responsibilities become mixed.

Advanced

Keep rendering pure and move side effects into hooks.

Mastery

Design components around product concepts: LessonCard, ProgressPanel, UserMenu, not vague boxes.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Function components exist to package UI into reusable pieces. They are the basic building blocks of modern React apps. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Function Components, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
function LessonCard({ title }) {
  return <article>{title}</article>
}

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without components, every screen becomes one large file with repeated markup and tangled logic. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
// one giant App component with repeated markup for every lesson

Learning Ladder in Detail

Beginner Level - Build the First Mental Model

A component is a function that returns UI. At this stage, your job is not speed. Your job is to say what each line does in plain language. Use tiny inputs and tiny outputs. If you cannot predict the result before running it, make the example smaller.

beginner example
function Welcome() { return <h1>Hello</h1> }

Beginner practice: copy the example exactly, run it, then change one name and one value. Explain what changed and what stayed the same.

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Pass data through props and split components when responsibilities become mixed. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
function Button({ label, onClick }) {
  return <button onClick={onClick}>{label}</button>
}

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Keep rendering pure and move side effects into hooks. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
function UserMenu({ user, onLogout }) { ... }

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Design components around product concepts: LessonCard, ProgressPanel, UserMenu, not vague boxes. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
components named after product concepts: CourseShell, LessonCard, ProgressPanel

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - Function ComponentsGuided Practice

Create a component that accepts title, description, and status props, then render it three times with different data.

A function component receives props and returns UI. Beginners should make the output correct. Intermediate learners should split components when one component has too many responsibilities. Mastery means designing component boundaries around product meaning, not around arbitrary line counts.

Component Boundary Example

jsx
function LessonCard({ title, level, minutes }) {
  const isLong = minutes >= 45

  return (
    <article className="lesson-card">
      <h3>{title}</h3>
      <p>{level}</p>
      <small>{isLong ? 'Deep focus' : 'Quick practice'}</small>
    </article>
  )
}

The modern way to write React components. A component is simply a function that returns JSX.

jsx
// Simplest possible component
function Greeting() {
  return <h1>Hello, React!</h1>
}

// Arrow function syntax (equally valid)
const Button = () => (
  <button>Click me</button>
)

// Render inside App:
function App() {
  return (
    <div>
      <Greeting />
      <Button />
    </div>
  )
}

Component Composition

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Composition exists so small components can be combined into larger interfaces without inheritance or duplication.

What if it did not exist?

Without composition, components become huge or overly configurable because every variation must be handled in one place.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Put components inside other components just like HTML elements.

Intermediate

Pass children when the parent controls layout but the child content varies.

Advanced

Use composition to separate layout, data, and behavior.

Mastery

Prefer clear composition over prop explosions. If a component has too many mode props, composition may be the answer.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Composition exists so small components can be combined into larger interfaces without inheritance or duplication. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Component Composition, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
// Component Composition
const example = "small practical use"

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without composition, components become huge or overly configurable because every variation must be handled in one place. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
// code becomes more repetitive, less clear, and harder to change

Prefer clear composition over prop explosions. If a component has too many mode props, composition may be the answer. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
choose it only when it improves clarity and maintainability

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - Component CompositionGuided Practice

Build a Card component that accepts children, then compose ProfileCard and LessonCard inside it.

React apps are built by composing small components into larger ones. This is the heart of React's component model.

jsx
// Small, focused components
function Avatar({ src, alt }) {
  return <img src={src} alt={alt} style={{ borderRadius: '50%', width: 60 }} />
}

function UserInfo({ name, role }) {
  return (
    <div>
      <strong>{name}</strong>
      <span>{role}</span>
    </div>
  )
}

// Composed together
function ProfileCard({ user }) {
  return (
    <div className="card">
      <Avatar src={user.avatar} alt={user.name} />
      <UserInfo name={user.name} role={user.role} />
    </div>
  )
}

Rendering Lists

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

List rendering exists because apps display collections: lessons, users, posts, todos, messages, products, and search results.

What if it did not exist?

Without list rendering, you would manually write repeated JSX for every item and the UI could not adapt to changing data.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use map to turn data items into components.

Intermediate

Use stable keys so React can track items correctly across changes.

Advanced

Handle empty, loading, filtered, and sorted lists intentionally.

Mastery

Separate data preparation from rendering when list logic grows.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

List rendering exists because apps display collections: lessons, users, posts, todos, messages, products, and search results. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Rendering Lists, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

intermediate example

Practice Ladder - Rendering ListsGuided Practice

Render a list of lessons, add stable keys, show an empty state, and filter by completed status.

Use Array.map() to render lists. Always provide a unique key prop.

jsx
const fruits = [
  { id: 1, name: 'Apple', emoji: '🍎' },
  { id: 2, name: 'Banana', emoji: '🍌' },
  { id: 3, name: 'Cherry', emoji: '🍒' },
]

function FruitList() {
  return (
    <ul>
      {fruits.map(fruit => (
        <li key={fruit.id}>
          {fruit.emoji} {fruit.name}
        </li>
      ))}
    </ul>
  )
}

// Conditional rendering
function StatusBadge({ isOnline }) {
  return (
    <span style={{ color: isOnline ? 'green' : 'gray' }}>
      {isOnline ? '● Online' : '○ Offline'}
    </span>
  )
}
🚨 Common Mistake — Keys
Never use array indices as keys if the list can be reordered or filtered. Use a unique, stable ID from your data instead.
Exercise 3.1 — Build a Card Component Day 3 · Exercise 1

Create a ProductCard component that displays a product name, price, and a "Buy" button. Render 3 different products in App.jsx.

jsx — ProductCard.jsx
// src/components/ProductCard.jsx
function ProductCard({ name, price }) {
  return (
    <div style={{ border: '1px solid #ccc', padding: 16, margin: 8, borderRadius: 8 }}>
      <h3>{name}</h3>
      <p>${price.toFixed(2)}</p>
      <button>Buy Now</button>
    </div>
  )
}
export default ProductCard

// src/App.jsx
import ProductCard from './components/ProductCard'

const products = [
  { id: 1, name: 'Laptop', price: 999.99 },
  { id: 2, name: 'Headphones', price: 149.99 },
  { id: 3, name: 'Keyboard', price: 79.99 },
]

function App() {
  return (
    <div>
      {products.map(p => (
        <ProductCard key={p.id} name={p.name} price={p.price} />
      ))}
    </div>
  )
}
Exercise 3.2 — Conditional Rendering Day 3 · Exercise 2

Build a WeatherWidget component that receives temperature (number) and isRaining (boolean). Display a sun emoji ☀️ if not raining and temp > 20, a cloud emoji ☁️ if not raining and cooler, or a rain emoji 🌧️ if raining.

jsx
function WeatherWidget({ temperature, isRaining }) {
  let icon
  if (isRaining) {
    icon = '🌧️'
  } else if (temperature > 20) {
    icon = '☀️'
  } else {
    icon = '☁️'
  }

  return (
    <div>
      <span style={{ fontSize: '3rem' }}>{icon}</span>
      <p>{temperature}°C — {isRaining ? 'Rainy' : 'Dry'}</p>
    </div>
  )
}

// Usage:
<WeatherWidget temperature={25} isRaining={false} />  // ☀️
<WeatherWidget temperature={14} isRaining={false} />  // ☁️
<WeatherWidget temperature={18} isRaining={true}  />  // 🌧️
Day 3 — Deep Dive

JSX Deep Dive

Expressions, fragments, portals, dynamic styling, and rendering patterns.

JSX Expressions Masterclass

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

JSX expressions exist so UI can respond to data. Anything inside braces is JavaScript expression land.

What if it did not exist?

Without expressions, JSX would be static markup and React could not show changing values.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use braces for values: {name}, {count + 1}, {isOpen ? ... : ...}.

Intermediate

Use variables for complex expressions so the JSX stays readable.

Advanced

Know the difference between expressions and statements: if is not directly returned inside braces, but ternaries are expressions.

Mastery

Make UI states obvious and avoid clever one-liners that future readers must decode.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

JSX expressions exist so UI can respond to data. Anything inside braces is JavaScript expression land. Practice Ladder - JSX Expressions MasterclassGuided Practice

Display a computed label, conditionally render a warning, and extract a complex expression into a variable.

jsx
function JsxExamples() {
  const items = ['Apple', 'Banana', 'Cherry']
  const user = { name: 'Alice', pro: true }
  const styles = { color: 'rebeccapurple', fontWeight: 700 }

  return (
    <div>
      {/* String expressions */}
      <p>{user.name.toUpperCase()}</p>

      {/* Ternary: render one of two things */}
      <p>{user.pro ? '⭐ Pro Member' : 'Free Member'}</p>

      {/* Short-circuit: render or nothing */}
      {user.pro && <span>Pro Badge</span>}

      {/* Inline styles are objects in JSX */}
      <p style={styles}>Styled text</p>

      {/* Template literal class names */}
      <div className={`card ${user.pro ? 'card--pro' : ''}`}>Card</div>

      {/* Rendering arrays */}
      <ul>
        {items.map((item, i) => <li key={i}>{item}</li>)}
      </ul>

      {/* IIFE for complex logic */}
      {(() => {
        if (user.pro) return <span>VIP</span>
        return <span>Basic</span>
      })()}
    </div>
  )
}

React Fragments

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Fragments exist to group multiple JSX elements without adding extra DOM nodes.

What if it did not exist?

Without fragments, you add unnecessary wrapper divs that can break layout, styling, or semantic HTML.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use <>...</> when a component must return siblings.

Intermediate

Use named <Fragment> when a key is needed in a list.

Advanced

Use fragments to preserve table, list, and flex/grid semantics.

Mastery

Keep DOM output clean and meaningful, not just visually correct.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Fragments exist to group multiple JSX elements without adding extra DOM nodes. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Return a heading and paragraph from one component without adding a wrapper div.

Fragments let you group elements without adding an extra DOM node — useful when a parent element is not allowed or not desirable.

jsx
// Shorthand (most common)
return (
  <>
    <h1>Title</h1>
    <p>Body</p>
  </>
)

// Named (needed when key prop is required)
return items.map(item => (
  <React.Fragment key={item.id}>
    <dt>{item.term}</dt>
    <dd>{item.definition}</dd>
  </React.Fragment>
))

React Portals

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Portals exist to render UI outside the normal parent DOM hierarchy while keeping React ownership.

What if it did not exist?

Without portals, modals, tooltips, and overlays can be trapped by parent overflow, z-index, or layout rules.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Think of a portal as "render this component over there in the DOM."

Intermediate

Use portals for modals and overlay layers.

Advanced

Manage focus, escape key behavior, and scroll locking for accessible overlays.

Mastery

Build overlays that are visually layered and semantically accessible.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Practice Ladder - React PortalsGuided Practice

Render a modal into a separate #modal-root and close it with a button.

Portals render children into a different DOM node than the parent component. Perfect for modals, tooltips, and notifications that need to break out of overflow:hidden containers.

jsx
import { createPortal } from 'react-dom'

function Modal({ isOpen, onClose, children }) {
  if (!isOpen) return null

  // Renders outside the React tree, directly into #modal-root
  return createPortal(
    <div className="modal-overlay" onClick={onClose}>
      <div className="modal-box" onClick={e => e.stopPropagation()}>
        {children}
        <button onClick={onClose}>Close</button>
      </div>
    </div>,
    document.getElementById('modal-root')  // target DOM node
  )
}

// index.html needs: <div id="modal-root"></div>

PropTypes — Runtime Type Checking

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

PropTypes exist to warn during development when components receive props of the wrong type.

What if it did not exist?

Without runtime checks or TypeScript, prop mistakes can silently create broken UI.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use PropTypes to document what props a component expects.

Intermediate

Mark required props and validate arrays, objects, and functions.

Advanced

Understand PropTypes check runtime values, while TypeScript checks during development/build.

Mastery

Use the right contract tool for the project: PropTypes for lightweight runtime docs, TypeScript for stronger app-wide guarantees.
