Practice Ladder - PropTypes — Runtime Type CheckingGuided Practice

Add PropTypes to a LessonCard requiring title, minutes, and completed.

bash + jsx
npm install prop-types

import PropTypes from 'prop-types'

function UserCard({ name, age, email, isAdmin }) {
  return <div>{name} ({age}){isAdmin && ' [Admin]'}</div>
}

UserCard.propTypes = {
  name: PropTypes.string.isRequired,
  age: PropTypes.number.isRequired,
  email: PropTypes.string,
  isAdmin: PropTypes.bool,
}

UserCard.defaultProps = {
  isAdmin: false,
  email: '',
}
TypeScript Alternative
In TypeScript projects you define component prop types with interfaces or type aliases instead of PropTypes — you get compile-time checks plus IDE autocompletion with zero runtime overhead.
Exercise — Dynamic Form BuilderDay 3B · Exercise

Build a DynamicForm component that receives a fields prop — an array of objects like { name, label, type, required }. Render the correct input for each field type (text, email, number, checkbox). Show a submit button and log the form values on submit.

jsx
import { useState } from 'react'

function DynamicForm({ fields }) {
  const [values, setValues] = useState(
    () => Object.fromEntries(
      fields.map(f => [f.name, f.type === 'checkbox' ? false : ''])
    )
  )

  const handleChange = (e) => {
    const { name, value, type, checked } = e.target
    setValues(prev => ({
      ...prev,
      [name]: type === 'checkbox' ? checked : value
    }))
  }

  const handleSubmit = (e) => {
    e.preventDefault()
    console.log(values)
  }

  return (
    <form onSubmit={handleSubmit}>
      {fields.map(field => (
        <div key={field.name}>
          <label>{field.label}{field.required && ' *'}</label>
          <input
            type={field.type}
            name={field.name}
            required={field.required}
            checked={field.type === 'checkbox' ? values[field.name] : undefined}
            value={field.type !== 'checkbox' ? values[field.name] : undefined}
            onChange={handleChange}
          />
        </div>
      ))}
      <button type="submit">Submit</button>
    </form>
  )
}

// Usage:
<DynamicForm fields={[
  { name: 'username', label: 'Username', type: 'text', required: true },
  { name: 'email', label: 'Email', type: 'email', required: true },
  { name: 'age', label: 'Age', type: 'number', required: false },
  { name: 'newsletter', label: 'Subscribe', type: 'checkbox', required: false },
]} />
Day 4

Props, State & Events

Making components dynamic and interactive.

Props — Passing Data Down

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Props exist so parent components can pass data and callbacks to children.

What if it did not exist?

Without props, components cannot be reused with different content and every component must know too much about the app.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Props are like function arguments for components.

Intermediate

Pass only what the child needs, not the whole world.

Advanced

Use callback props when children need to report events upward.

Mastery

Design props as a clear public API for the component.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Props exist so parent components can pass data and callbacks to children. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Props — Passing Data Down, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
<LessonCard title="Hooks" minutes={45} />

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without props, components cannot be reused with different content and every component must know too much about the app. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
// child component must import global data or duplicate values

Learning Ladder in Detail

Beginner Level - Build the First Mental Model

Props are like function arguments for components. At this stage, your job is not speed. Your job is to say what each line does in plain language. Use tiny inputs and tiny outputs. If you cannot predict the result before running it, make the example smaller.

beginner example
function Hello({ name }) { return <p>Hello {name}</p> }

Beginner practice: copy the example exactly, run it, then change one name and one value. Explain what changed and what stayed the same.

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Pass only what the child needs, not the whole world. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
<Button label="Save" onClick={handleSave} />

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Use callback props when children need to report events upward. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
function EmptyState({ title, action }) { return <section>{title}{action}</section> }

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Design props as a clear public API for the component. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
small prop API: exact data in, clear events out

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - Props — Passing Data DownGuided Practice

Create a Button component with label, variant, and onClick props.

Props are how a parent component gives information to a child component. At beginner level, props feel like function parameters. At mastery level, props are a component contract: they define what the child needs, what it can render, and what it should not know.

Props Contract Example

jsx
function CourseProgress({ completed, total }) {
  const percent = Math.round((completed / total) * 100)

  return (
    <section>
      <strong>{percent}% complete</strong>
      <progress value={completed} max={total} />
    </section>
  )
}

The child receives numbers, not the entire course object. This makes the component easier to reuse and easier to test.

Props (properties) let you pass data from a parent component to a child component. They are read-only — a component must never modify its own props.

jsx
// Child component receives props as parameter
function Greeting({ name, age }) {
  return <p>Hello, {name}! You are {age} years old.</p>
}

// Parent passes data as attributes
function App() {
  return (
    <div>
      <Greeting name="Alice" age={28} />
      <Greeting name="Bob" age={32} />
    </div>
  )
}

// Default props
function Button({ label = 'Click me', variant = 'primary' }) {
  return <button className={`btn btn-${variant}`}>{label}</button>
}

// children prop — anything between opening/closing tags
function Card({ children, title }) {
  return (
    <div className="card">
      <h2>{title}</h2>
      {children}
    </div>
  )
}
// Usage: <Card title="News"><p>Article content</p></Card>

State with useState

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

State exists to let a component remember information between renders.

What if it did not exist?

Without state, React components could show props but could not respond to typing, clicking, toggling, or loaded data.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use state for values that change because of user interaction or time.

Intermediate

Use functional updates when next state depends on previous state.

Advanced

Avoid duplicated state by deriving values during render.

Mastery

Keep state minimal, predictable, and close to where it is used.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

State exists to let a component remember information between renders. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with State with useState, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
const [count, setCount] = useState(0)
setCount(count + 1)

// UI will not automatically re-render

Learning Ladder in Detail

const [open, setOpen] = useState(false)

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

store minimal state; derive completedCount from todos instead of storing it separately

Practice Ladder - State with useStateGuided Practice

Build a counter, a show/hide toggle, and a todo list item update without mutation.

State is component memory. The key beginner rule is: do not change state directly. The professional rule is: store the minimum state needed and derive everything else during render.

State Update Progression

jsx
// Beginner: simple counter
const [count, setCount] = useState(0)
setCount(count + 1)

// Safer when next state depends on current state
setCount(current => current + 1)

// Object state: copy, then change the field
setProfile(current => ({ ...current, name: 'Naveed' }))

// Array state: map to update one item
setLessons(current =>
  current.map(lesson =>
    lesson.id === id ? { ...lesson, done: true } : lesson
  )
)

State is local data that belongs to a component. When state changes, React re-renders the component automatically.

jsx
import { useState } from 'react'

function Counter() {
  // [currentValue, setterFunction] = useState(initialValue)
  const [count, setCount] = useState(0)

  return (
    <div>
      <p>Count: {count}</p>
      <button onClick={() => setCount(count + 1)}>+</button>
      <button onClick={() => setCount(count - 1)}>-</button>
      <button onClick={() => setCount(0)}>Reset</button>
    </div>
  )
}

Functional Updates (Important!)

⚠️ Always use functional updates when new state depends on old state
This prevents bugs caused by stale closures, especially in async code.
jsx
// ❌ Wrong — may use stale state
setCount(count + 1)

// ✅ Correct — always gets latest state
setCount(prev => prev + 1)

// For objects — ALWAYS spread to preserve other fields
const [user, setUser] = useState({ name: 'Alice', age: 28, city: 'NYC' })

// ❌ Wrong — replaces entire object
setUser({ name: 'Bob' })

// ✅ Correct — merges changes
setUser(prev => ({ ...prev, name: 'Bob' }))

Handling Events

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Events exist so user actions can change application state or trigger behavior.

What if it did not exist?

Without event handling, the UI is only a static display and cannot react to clicks, typing, submitting, or keyboard actions.

Beginner

Attach handlers like onClick and onChange.

Intermediate

Advanced

Understand event propagation and when to stop it.

Mastery

Design interactions that are predictable, accessible, and easy to test.

Practice Ladder - Handling EventsGuided Practice

Handle a button click, an input change, a form submit, and an escape key close action.

jsx
function FormExample() {
  const [text, setText] = useState('')
  const [submitted, setSubmitted] = useState(null)

  const handleChange = (e) => {
    setText(e.target.value)
  }

  const handleSubmit = (e) => {
    e.preventDefault()   // prevent page reload
    setSubmitted(text)
    setText('')
  }

  return (
    <form onSubmit={handleSubmit}>
      <input
        value={text}
        onChange={handleChange}
        placeholder="Type something..."
      />
      <button type="submit">Submit</button>
      {submitted && <p>You submitted: {submitted}</p>}
    </form>
  )
}

Lifting State Up

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Lifting state exists so multiple components can share and coordinate the same changing value.

What if it did not exist?

Without lifting state, sibling components drift out of sync or duplicate the same data separately.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Move state to the nearest common parent when two children need it.

Intermediate

Pass the current value down and callbacks back up.

Advanced

Know when lifted state has grown enough to need context, URL state, or a store.

Mastery

Place state at the smallest level that makes the workflow coherent.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Lifting state exists so multiple components can share and coordinate the same changing value. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Practice Ladder - Lifting State UpGuided Practice

Build a filter input and list component that share search text through their parent.

When two sibling components need to share state, move the state to their nearest common parent and pass it down as props.

jsx
// TemperatureDisplay just shows the value
function TemperatureDisplay({ temp }) {
  return <p>Temperature: {temp}°C</p>
}

// TemperatureControl just raises/lowers it
function TemperatureControl({ onIncrease, onDecrease }) {
  return (
    <div>
      <button onClick={onIncrease}>🔥 Hotter</button>
      <button onClick={onDecrease}>❄️ Cooler</button>
    </div>
  )
}

// Parent owns the state
function Thermostat() {
  const [temp, setTemp] = useState(20)

  return (
    <div>
      <TemperatureDisplay temp={temp} />
      <TemperatureControl
        onIncrease={() => setTemp(t => t + 1)}
        onDecrease={() => setTemp(t => t - 1)}
      />
    </div>
  )
}
Exercise 4.1 — Shopping Cart Day 4 · Exercise 1

Build a mini shopping cart: display a list of items with names and prices. Each item has an "Add to Cart" button. Show a cart total that updates when items are added. Also show a count of items in the cart.

jsx
import { useState } from 'react'

const ITEMS = [
  { id: 1, name: 'Coffee', price: 3.5 },
  { id: 2, name: 'Sandwich', price: 6.99 },
  { id: 3, name: 'Juice', price: 2.5 },
]

function ShoppingCart() {
  const [cart, setCart] = useState([])

  const addToCart = (item) => {
    setCart(prev => [...prev, item])
  }

  const total = cart.reduce((sum, item) => sum + item.price, 0)

  return (
    <div>
      <h2>Menu</h2>
      {ITEMS.map(item => (
        <div key={item.id}>
          <span>{item.name} — ${item.price}</span>
          <button onClick={() => addToCart(item)}>Add</button>
        </div>
      ))}
      <hr />
      <h2>Cart ({cart.length} items)</h2>
      <p>Total: ${total.toFixed(2)}</p>
    </div>
  )
}

All Input Types as Controlled Components

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Controlled components exist so form values live in React state and the UI always reflects that state.

What if it did not exist?

Without controlled forms, validation, conditional fields, and submit logic become harder to coordinate.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Bind value to state and update it with onChange.

Intermediate

Handle checkboxes, radios, selects, and textareas with the correct value shape.

Advanced

Centralize field updates and validation for larger forms.

Mastery

Design forms as data models with clear validation and submission boundaries.

Practice Ladder - All Input Types as Controlled ComponentsGuided Practice

Build a signup form with text, email, checkbox, select, validation, and submit output.

A controlled input gets its value from React state and reports changes back through an event. Beginners should wire one text input. Intermediate learners should handle checkboxes, selects, and radio groups. Mastery means designing one clear form state shape before rendering inputs.

Form State Shape Example

jsx
const [values, setValues] = useState({
  name: '',
  email: '',
  level: 'beginner',
  wantsUpdates: true
})

function updateField(name, value) {
  setValues(current => ({ ...current, [name]: value }))
}

This pattern keeps form updates consistent. Every field uses the same pathway, which makes validation and submission easier to reason about.

jsx
import { useState } from 'react'

function AllInputsDemo() {
  const [form, setForm] = useState({
    text: '',
    email: '',
    password: '',
    number: '',
    textarea: '',
    select: 'option1',
    checkbox: false,
    radio: 'male',
    range: 50,
  })

  // Universal change handler using computed property names
  const handleChange = ({ target }) => {
    const { name, value, type, checked } = target
    setForm(prev => ({
      ...prev,
      [name]: type === 'checkbox' ? checked : value
    }))
  }

  return (
    <form>
      {/* Text */}
      <input name="text" type="text" value={form.text} onChange={handleChange} />

      {/* Textarea */}
      <textarea name="textarea" value={form.textarea} onChange={handleChange} />

      {/* Select */}
      <select name="select" value={form.select} onChange={handleChange}>
        <option value="option1">Option 1</option>
        <option value="option2">Option 2</option>
      </select>

      {/* Checkbox — uses 'checked' not 'value' */}
      <input name="checkbox" type="checkbox" checked={form.checkbox} onChange={handleChange} />

      {/* Radio group */}
      <input name="radio" type="radio" value="male"   checked={form.radio === 'male'}   onChange={handleChange} />
      <input name="radio" type="radio" value="female" checked={form.radio === 'female'} onChange={handleChange} />

      {/* Range slider */}
      <input name="range" type="range" min="0" max="100" value={form.range} onChange={handleChange} />
      <span>{form.range}</span>
    </form>
  )
}

Manual Validation Pattern

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Manual validation exists to teach the rules behind form libraries. It makes errors explicit and testable.

What if it did not exist?

Without validation, users can submit incomplete or incorrect data and the app must recover later.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Write one function that receives values and returns errors.

Intermediate

Validate on submit first, then add touched-field validation for better UX.

Advanced

Separate client validation from server validation and display both clearly.

Mastery

Make validation predictable, accessible, and easy to test.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Manual validation exists to teach the rules behind form libraries. It makes errors explicit and testable. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Practice Ladder - Manual Validation PatternGuided Practice

Validate name, email, password length, and matching confirm password fields.

jsx
function SignupForm() {
  const [fields, setFields] = useState({ email: '', password: '', confirm: '' })
  const [errors, setErrors] = useState({})
  const [submitted, setSubmitted] = useState(false)

  const validate = (values) => {
    const errs = {}
    if (!values.email.includes('@')) errs.email = 'Enter a valid email'
    if (values.password.length < 8) errs.password = 'Password must be 8+ chars'
    if (values.password !== values.confirm) errs.confirm = 'Passwords do not match'
    return errs
  }

  const handleChange = ({ target: { name, value } }) => {
    setFields(prev => ({ ...prev, [name]: value }))
    // Clear error for this field as user types
    setErrors(prev => ({ ...prev, [name]: '' }))
  }

  const handleSubmit = (e) => {
    e.preventDefault()
    const errs = validate(fields)
    if (Object.keys(errs).length) { setErrors(errs); return }
    setSubmitted(true)
    console.log('Submit:', fields)
  }

  if (submitted) return <p>✓ Account created for {fields.email}!</p>

  return (
    <form onSubmit={handleSubmit}>
      {['email', 'password', 'confirm'].map(field => (
        <div key={field}>
          <label>{field}</label>
          <input
            name={field}
            type={field === 'email' ? 'email' : 'password'}
            value={fields[field]}
            onChange={handleChange}
            style={{ borderColor: errors[field] ? 'red' : '' }}
          />
          {errors[field] && <span style={{color:'red', fontSize:'0.85rem'}}>{errors[field]}</span>}
        </div>
      ))}
      <button type="submit">Create Account</button>
    </form>
  )
}

React Hook Form — Production Approach

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

React Hook Form exists to manage complex form state with less re-rendering and less boilerplate.

What if it did not exist?

Without a form library, large forms can become repetitive and difficult to maintain.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use it after you understand controlled inputs and validation basics.

Intermediate

Register fields, show errors, and handle submit through the library.

Advanced

Integrate schema validation, dynamic fields, and async submission states.

Mastery

Choose a form library when complexity justifies it, not because every form needs one.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

React Hook Form exists to manage complex form state with less re-rendering and less boilerplate. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with React Hook Form — Production Approach, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
<input value={email} onChange={e => setEmail(e.target.value)} />

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without a form library, large forms can become repetitive and difficult to maintain. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
// form values drift away from React state

Learning Ladder in Detail

Beginner Level - Build the First Mental Model

Use it after you understand controlled inputs and validation basics. At this stage, your job is not speed. Your job is to say what each line does in plain language. Use tiny inputs and tiny outputs. If you cannot predict the result before running it, make the example smaller.

beginner example
control one input

Beginner practice: copy the example exactly, run it, then change one name and one value. Explain what changed and what stayed the same.

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Register fields, show errors, and handle submit through the library. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
validate on submit

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Integrate schema validation, dynamic fields, and async submission states. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
show field errors accessibly

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Choose a form library when complexity justifies it, not because every form needs one. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
form data model, validation, submit lifecycle

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - React Hook Form — Production ApproachGuided Practice

Rebuild a manual signup form using React Hook Form and compare the code.

bash + jsx
npm install react-hook-form @hookform/resolvers zod

import { useForm } from 'react-hook-form'
import { zodResolver } from '@hookform/resolvers/zod'
import { z } from 'zod'

const schema = z.object({
  email:    z.string().email('Enter a valid email'),
  password: z.string().min(8, 'Password must be 8+ chars'),
  confirm:  z.string(),
}).refine(d => d.password === d.confirm, {
  message: 'Passwords do not match',
  path: ['confirm'],
})

function SignupRHF() {
  const {
    register,
    handleSubmit,
    formState: { errors, isSubmitting }
  } = useForm({ resolver: zodResolver(schema) })

  const onSubmit = async (data) => {
    await new Promise(r => setTimeout(r, 1000))
    console.log(data)
  }

  return (
    <form onSubmit={handleSubmit(onSubmit)}>
      <input {...register('email')}    placeholder="Email" />
      {errors.email    && <p>{errors.email.message}</p>}

      <input {...register('password')} type="password" placeholder="Password" />
      {errors.password && <p>{errors.password.message}</p>}

      <input {...register('confirm')}  type="password" placeholder="Confirm" />
      {errors.confirm  && <p>{errors.confirm.message}</p>}

      <button type="submit" disabled={isSubmitting}>
        {isSubmitting ? 'Saving...' : 'Create Account'}
      </button>
    </form>
  )
}
Exercise 4B.1 — Multi-Step FormDay 4B · Exercise

Build a 3-step registration form. Step 1: Name and email. Step 2: Password (with confirmation). Step 3: Review all details and confirm. Include Next/Back navigation, validate each step before proceeding, and show a progress indicator (Step 1 of 3).

jsx
import { useState } from 'react'

const STEPS = ['Your Info', 'Security', 'Review']

function MultiStepForm() {
  const [step, setStep] = useState(0)
  const [data, setData] = useState({
    name: '', email: '', password: '', confirm: ''
  })
  const [errors, setErrors] = useState({})
  const [done, setDone] = useState(false)

  const update = ({ target: { name, value } }) =>
    setData(p => ({ ...p, [name]: value }))

  const validateStep = () => {
    const e = {}
    if (step === 0) {
      if (!data.name.trim()) e.name = 'Name required'
      if (!data.email.includes('@')) e.email = 'Valid email required'
    }
    if (step === 1) {
      if (data.password.length < 8) e.password = '8+ chars required'
      if (data.password !== data.confirm) e.confirm = 'Passwords must match'
    }
    setErrors(e)
    return Object.keys(e).length === 0
  }

  const next = () => { if (validateStep()) setStep(s => s + 1) }
  const back = () => { setErrors({}); setStep(s => s - 1) }

  if (done) return <p>✓ Welcome, {data.name}!</p>

  return (
    <div style={{maxWidth:400, margin:'auto'}}>
      {/* Progress */}
      <p>Step {step + 1} of {STEPS.length}: {STEPS[step]}</p>
      <div style={{display:'flex',gap:4,marginBottom:16}}>
        {STEPS.map((_, i) => (
          <div key={i} style={{flex:1,height:4,borderRadius:2,
            background: i <= step ? '#c8860a' : '#e5e5e5'}} />
        ))}
      </div>

      {step === 0 && (
        <>
          <input name="name"  value={data.name}  onChange={update} placeholder="Full name" />
          {errors.name  && <p style={{color:'red'}}>{errors.name}</p>}
          <input name="email" value={data.email} onChange={update} placeholder="Email" />
          {errors.email && <p style={{color:'red'}}>{errors.email}</p>}
        </>
      )}
      {step === 1 && (
        <>
          <input name="password" type="password" value={data.password} onChange={update} placeholder="Password" />
          {errors.password && <p style={{color:'red'}}>{errors.password}</p>}
          <input name="confirm"  type="password" value={data.confirm}  onChange={update} placeholder="Confirm password" />
          {errors.confirm  && <p style={{color:'red'}}>{errors.confirm}</p>}
        </>
      )}
      {step === 2 && (
        <div>
          <p>👤 Name: {data.name}</p>
          <p>📧 Email: {data.email}</p>
          <p>🔒 Password: {'*'.repeat(data.password.length)}</p>
        </div>
      )}

      <div style={{display:'flex',gap:8,marginTop:16}}>
        {step > 0 && <button type="button" onClick={back}>← Back</button>}
        {step < 2
          ? <button type="button" onClick={next}>Next →</button>
          : <button type="button" onClick={() => setDone(true)}>✓ Confirm</button>
        }
      </div>
    </div>
  )
}
Day 5

Hooks Deep Dive

useEffect, useReducer, useRef, useContext, and building custom hooks.

useEffect — Side Effects

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

useEffect exists to synchronize React with systems outside rendering: network, timers, subscriptions, storage, and browser APIs.

What if it did not exist?

Without effects, components could render data but could not safely connect to external systems after render.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use effects for things that happen because the component appeared or because a dependency changed.

Intermediate

Declare dependencies honestly and add cleanup when the effect creates something long-lived.

Advanced

Avoid effects for derived values; calculate those during render.

Mastery

Write effects as synchronization processes with setup, dependency, and cleanup clearly defined.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

useEffect exists to synchronize React with systems outside rendering: network, timers, subscriptions, storage, and browser APIs. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with useEffect — Side Effects, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
useEffect(() => {
  document.title = title
}, [title])

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without effects, components could render data but could not safely connect to external systems after render. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
// document title, timers, subscriptions, and fetches run in random places

Learning Ladder in Detail

Beginner Level - Build the First Mental Model

Use effects for things that happen because the component appeared or because a dependency changed. At this stage, your job is not speed. Your job is to say what each line does in plain language. Use tiny inputs and tiny outputs. If you cannot predict the result before running it, make the example smaller.

beginner example
useEffect(() => { console.log("mounted") }, [])

Beginner practice: copy the example exactly, run it, then change one name and one value. Explain what changed and what stayed the same.

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Declare dependencies honestly and add cleanup when the effect creates something long-lived. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
useEffect(() => {
  fetchUsers()
}, [])

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Avoid effects for derived values; calculate those during render. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
useEffect(() => {
  const id = setInterval(tick, 1000)
  return () => clearInterval(id)
}, [])

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Write effects as synchronization processes with setup, dependency, and cleanup clearly defined. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
effects synchronize external systems; derived values stay in render

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - useEffect — Side EffectsGuided Practice

Set document title, start and clean a timer, and fetch data with loading and error states.

useEffect synchronizes React with systems outside React: browser APIs, network requests, timers, subscriptions, and document title changes. If you are only calculating a value for render, you probably do not need an effect.

Effect Decision Examples

jsx
// Not an effect: derive during render
const completed = lessons.filter(lesson => lesson.done).length

// Effect: synchronize with browser document title
useEffect(() => {
  document.title = completed + ' lessons complete'
}, [completed])

// Effect with cleanup: timer
useEffect(() => {
  const id = setInterval(refreshProgress, 30000)
  return () => clearInterval(id)
}, [])

useEffect lets you run code after a render — for things like API calls, subscriptions, or manually changing the DOM.

jsx
import { useState, useEffect } from 'react'

function DataFetcher() {
  const [users, setUsers] = useState([])
  const [loading, setLoading] = useState(true)
  const [error, setError] = useState(null)

  useEffect(() => {
    // This runs after the component mounts
    let cancelled = false

    fetch('https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/users')
      .then(res => res.json())
      .then(data => {
        if (!cancelled) {
          setUsers(data)
          setLoading(false)
        }
      })
      .catch(err => {
        if (!cancelled) setError(err.message)
      })

    // Cleanup function — runs on unmount or before re-run
    return () => { cancelled = true }
  }, []) // ← dependency array: empty = run once on mount

  if (loading) return <p>Loading...</p>
  if (error) return <p>Error: {error}</p>

  return (
    <ul>
      {users.map(u => <li key={u.id}>{u.name}</li>)}
    </ul>
  )
}

useEffect Dependency Rules

No array

Runs after every render. Rarely what you want.

Empty array []

Runs once on mount, cleanup on unmount.

With deps [a, b]

Runs on mount and whenever a or b change.

useReducer — Complex State

Intermediate

Advanced

Keep reducers pure and test them separately from React.

Mastery

Use reducers to make state machines and business rules explicit.

Practice Ladder - useReducer — Complex StateGuided Practice

Build a reducer for a cart with add item, remove item, change quantity, and clear cart actions.

Use useReducer instead of multiple useState calls when state logic is complex or actions have multiple steps.

jsx
import { useReducer } from 'react'

const initialState = { count: 0, step: 1 }

function reducer(state, action) {
  switch (action.type) {
    case 'increment':
      return { ...state, count: state.count + state.step }
    case 'decrement':
      return { ...state, count: state.count - state.step }
    case 'setStep':
      return { ...state, step: action.payload }
    case 'reset':
      return initialState
    default:
      return state
  }
}

function AdvancedCounter() {
  const [state, dispatch] = useReducer(reducer, initialState)

  return (
    <div>
      <p>Count: {state.count} (step: {state.step})</p>
      <button onClick={() => dispatch({ type: 'increment' })}>+</button>
      <button onClick={() => dispatch({ type: 'decrement' })}>-</button>
      <button onClick={() => dispatch({ type: 'reset' })}>Reset</button>
      <input
        type="number"
        value={state.step}
        onChange={e => dispatch({ type: 'setStep', payload: +e.target.value })}
      />
    </div>
  )
}

useRef — Mutable Refs

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

useRef exists to keep a mutable value that does not trigger a re-render, or to access a DOM node.

What if it did not exist?

Without refs, focus management, measuring elements, and storing non-render values become awkward.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use refs to focus an input.

Intermediate

Use refs for timers, previous values, or DOM measurements.

Advanced

Avoid using refs as hidden state that should actually update the UI.

Mastery

Use refs for escape hatches while keeping the main data flow declarative.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Practice Ladder - useRef — Mutable RefsGuided Practice

Focus an input on button click and store an interval id in a ref for cleanup.

jsx
import { useRef } from 'react'

function FocusInput() {
  const inputRef = useRef(null)

  const focusIt = () => {
    inputRef.current.focus()
  }

  return (
    <div>
      <input ref={inputRef} placeholder="Click the button to focus me" />
      <button onClick={focusIt}>Focus Input</button>
    </div>
  )
}

// useRef for storing mutable values (won't trigger re-render)
function RenderCounter() {
  const renderCount = useRef(0)
  renderCount.current += 1
  return <p>Rendered {renderCount.current} times</p>
}

useContext — Avoid Prop Drilling

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Context exists to share values across a component tree without passing props through every level.

What if it did not exist?

Without context, theme, locale, auth user, or settings may be threaded through components that do not use them.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use context for values many descendants need.

Intermediate

Split contexts when unrelated values change at different rates.

Advanced

Avoid putting constantly changing large state in one broad context.

Mastery

Use context for dependency access and app-level values, not as a default replacement for all props.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Context exists to share values across a component tree without passing props through every level. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with useContext — Avoid Prop Drilling, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - useContext — Avoid Prop DrillingGuided Practice

Create a ThemeContext and consume it from a deeply nested button.

jsx
import { createContext, useContext, useState } from 'react'

// 1. Create the context
const ThemeContext = createContext('light')

// 2. Provide it high up in the tree
function App() {
  const [theme, setTheme] = useState('light')
  return (
    <ThemeContext.Provider value={{ theme, setTheme }}>
      <Page />
    </ThemeContext.Provider>
  )
}

// 3. Consume anywhere in the tree — no prop drilling!
function ThemeToggle() {
  const { theme, setTheme } = useContext(ThemeContext)
  return (
    <button onClick={() => setTheme(t => t === 'light' ? 'dark' : 'light')}>
      Current: {theme} — Toggle
    </button>
  )
}

Custom Hooks

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Custom hooks exist to reuse stateful behavior between components.

What if it did not exist?

Without custom hooks, components copy the same effect/state logic repeatedly.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Extract a hook when two components use the same behavior.

Intermediate

Return named values and actions so the caller reads naturally.

Advanced

Hide implementation details but keep inputs and outputs clear.

Mastery

Build hooks that feel like small APIs: focused, documented by naming, and easy to test.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Custom hooks exist to reuse stateful behavior between components. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Custom Hooks, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
const [value, setValue] = useState(initialValue)

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without custom hooks, components copy the same effect/state logic repeatedly. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

Practice Ladder - Custom HooksGuided Practice

Create useLocalStorage, useToggle, and useDebounce hooks.

A custom hook extracts reusable stateful logic. It should not hide UI. If the repeated part is behavior, create a hook. If the repeated part is markup, create a component.

Custom Hook Design Example

javascript
function useToggle(initial = false) {
  const [value, setValue] = useState(initial)

  return {
    value,
    toggle: () => setValue(current => !current),
    setOn: () => setValue(true),
    setOff: () => setValue(false)
  }
}

Custom hooks let you extract reusable stateful logic. A custom hook is just a function whose name starts with use.

jsx — hooks/useFetch.js
import { useState, useEffect } from 'react'

export function useFetch(url) {
  const [data, setData] = useState(null)
  const [loading, setLoading] = useState(true)
  const [error, setError] = useState(null)

  useEffect(() => {
    let cancelled = false
    setLoading(true)

    fetch(url)
      .then(r => r.json())
      .then(d => { if (!cancelled) { setData(d); setLoading(false) } })
      .catch(e => { if (!cancelled) setError(e.message) })

    return () => { cancelled = true }
  }, [url])

  return { data, loading, error }
}

// Usage — clean and reusable
function UserList() {
  const { data, loading, error } = useFetch('https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/users')

  if (loading) return <p>Loading...</p>
  if (error) return <p>Error: {error}</p>
  return <ul>{data?.map(u => <li key={u.id}>{u.name}</li>)}</ul>
}
Exercise 5.1 — useLocalStorage Custom Hook Day 5 · Exercise 1

Build a custom hook useLocalStorage(key, initialValue) that works like useState but persists the value to localStorage. Test it with a name input that survives page refreshes.

jsx
import { useState } from 'react'

function useLocalStorage(key, initialValue) {
  const [value, setValue] = useState(() => {
    try {
      const item = window.localStorage.getItem(key)
      return item ? JSON.parse(item) : initialValue
    } catch {
      return initialValue
    }
  })

  const setStoredValue = (newValue) => {
    setValue(newValue)
    window.localStorage.setItem(key, JSON.stringify(newValue))
  }

  return [value, setStoredValue]
}

// Test component
function App() {
  const [name, setName] = useLocalStorage('username', '')

  return (
    <div>
      <input value={name} onChange={e => setName(e.target.value)} />
      <p>Hello, {name || 'stranger'}! (Refresh to verify)</p>
    </div>
  )
}

useDebounce

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

useDebounce exists to wait until fast-changing input settles before running expensive work.

What if it did not exist?

Without debouncing, search boxes and filters may trigger a request or calculation on every keystroke.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Delay a value by a small amount of time.

Intermediate

Use it for search, resize, and validation feedback.

Advanced

Cancel outdated work so old results do not overwrite new intent.

Mastery

Balance responsiveness with performance; do not debounce interactions that should feel immediate.

Practice Ladder - useDebounceGuided Practice

Create a search input that updates results only after the user stops typing for 400ms.

Delays executing a value update until the user stops typing — prevents excessive API calls on search inputs.

jsx — hooks/useDebounce.js
import { useState, useEffect } from 'react'

export function useDebounce(value, delay = 500) {
  const [debouncedValue, setDebouncedValue] = useState(value)

  useEffect(() => {
    const timer = setTimeout(() => {
      setDebouncedValue(value)
    }, delay)

    return () => clearTimeout(timer)
  }, [value, delay])

  return debouncedValue
}

// Usage in a search component
function Search() {
  const [query, setQuery] = useState('')
  const debouncedQuery = useDebounce(query, 400)

  useEffect(() => {
    if (debouncedQuery) {
      // API call only fires 400ms after user stops typing
      console.log('Searching for:', debouncedQuery)
    }
  }, [debouncedQuery])

  return <input value={query} onChange={e => setQuery(e.target.value)} />
}

Practice Ladder - useWindowSizeGuided Practice

Show compact labels when width is below 640px and full labels above it.

jsx — hooks/useWindowSize.js
import { useState, useEffect } from 'react'

export function useWindowSize() {
  const [size, setSize] = useState({
    width: window.innerWidth,
    height: window.innerHeight,
  })

  useEffect(() => {
    const handleResize = () => {
      setSize({ width: window.innerWidth, height: window.innerHeight })
    }
    window.addEventListener('resize', handleResize)
    return () => window.removeEventListener('resize', handleResize)
  }, [])

  return size
}

// Usage
function ResponsiveMenu() {
  const { width } = useWindowSize()
  return width < 768
    ? <MobileMenu />
    : <DesktopMenu />
}

Practice Ladder - usePreviousGuided Practice

Show whether a counter increased, decreased, or stayed the same since the previous render.

jsx — hooks/usePrevious.js
import { useRef, useEffect } from 'react'

export function usePrevious(value) {
  const ref = useRef(undefined)

  useEffect(() => {
    ref.current = value
  }, [value])

  return ref.current  // the value from the previous render
}

// Usage: show what changed
function ScoreBoard({ score }) {
  const prevScore = usePrevious(score)
  const diff = score - (prevScore || 0)

  return (
    <div>
      Score: {score}
      {diff > 0 && <span style={{color:'green'}}> +{diff}</span>}
    </div>
  )
}

Practice Ladder - useOnClickOutsideGuided Practice

Create a dropdown that closes on outside click and escape key.

jsx — hooks/useOnClickOutside.js
import { useEffect } from 'react'

export function useOnClickOutside(ref, handler) {
  useEffect(() => {
    const listener = (e) => {
      if (!ref.current || ref.current.contains(e.target)) return
      handler(e)
    }
    document.addEventListener('mousedown', listener)
    document.addEventListener('touchstart', listener)
    return () => {
      document.removeEventListener('mousedown', listener)
      document.removeEventListener('touchstart', listener)
    }
  }, [ref, handler])
}

// Usage: close dropdown on outside click
function Dropdown() {
  const [open, setOpen] = useState(false)
  const ref = useRef(null)

  useOnClickOutside(ref, () => setOpen(false))

  return (
    <div ref={ref}>
      <button onClick={() => setOpen(v => !v)}>Menu</button>
      {open && <ul><li>Option 1</li><li>Option 2</li></ul>}
    </div>
  )
}

Practice Ladder - useMediaQueryGuided Practice

Change a chart legend position based on a media query match.

jsx — hooks/useMediaQuery.js
import { useState, useEffect } from 'react'

export function useMediaQuery(query) {
  const [matches, setMatches] = useState(
    () => window.matchMedia(query).matches
  )

  useEffect(() => {
    const mq = window.matchMedia(query)
    const handler = (e) => setMatches(e.matches)
    mq.addEventListener('change', handler)
    return () => mq.removeEventListener('change', handler)
  }, [query])

  return matches
}

// Usage
const isMobile = useMediaQuery('(max-width: 768px)')
const prefersDark = useMediaQuery('(prefers-color-scheme: dark)')

Why it exists

Beginner

Track loading, data, and error.

Intermediate

Expose a run or refetch function.

Advanced

Ignore outdated requests when newer ones finish first.

Mastery

Know when a simple custom hook is enough and when React Query is a better server-state tool.

Example: when working with useAsync, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

Practice Ladder - useAsyncGuided Practice

Build useAsync around a fake API call and render all three states.

jsx — hooks/useAsync.js
import { useState, useCallback } from 'react'

export function useAsync(asyncFn) {
  const [state, setState] = useState({
    status: 'idle',  // idle | pending | success | error
    data: null,
    error: null,
  })

  const execute = useCallback(async (...args) => {
    setState({ status: 'pending', data: null, error: null })
    try {
      const data = await asyncFn(...args)
      setState({ status: 'success', data, error: null })
      return data
    } catch (error) {
      setState({ status: 'error', data: null, error })
      throw error
    }
  }, [asyncFn])

  return { ...state, execute,
    isIdle: state.status === 'idle',
    isLoading: state.status === 'pending',
    isSuccess: state.status === 'success',
    isError: state.status === 'error',
  }
}

// Usage
function CreatePost() {
  const { execute, isLoading, isSuccess, error } = useAsync(
    (data) => fetch('/api/posts', {
      method: 'POST',
      body: JSON.stringify(data),
    }).then(r => r.json())
  )

  return (
    <div>
      <button onClick={() => execute({ title: 'New Post' })} disabled={isLoading}>
        {isLoading ? 'Creating...' : 'Create Post'}
      </button>
      {isSuccess && <p>Post created!</p>}
      {error && <p>Error: {error.message}</p>}
    </div>
  )
}
Exercise — Build useTimer HookDay 5B · Exercise

Build a useTimer(initialSeconds) hook that tracks remaining seconds. It should expose: seconds, isRunning, start(), pause(), reset(). Build a timer UI component using this hook.

jsx
import { useState, useEffect, useRef, useCallback } from 'react'

function useTimer(initialSeconds = 60) {
  const [seconds, setSeconds] = useState(initialSeconds)
  const [isRunning, setIsRunning] = useState(false)
  const intervalRef = useRef(null)

  useEffect(() => {
    if (isRunning && seconds > 0) {
      intervalRef.current = setInterval(() => {
        setSeconds(s => s - 1)
      }, 1000)
    } else if (seconds === 0) {
      setIsRunning(false)
    }
    return () => clearInterval(intervalRef.current)
  }, [isRunning, seconds])

  const start  = useCallback(() => setIsRunning(true), [])
  const pause  = useCallback(() => setIsRunning(false), [])
  const reset  = useCallback(() => {
    setIsRunning(false)
    setSeconds(initialSeconds)
  }, [initialSeconds])

  return { seconds, isRunning, start, pause, reset }
}

function TimerApp() {
  const { seconds, isRunning, start, pause, reset } = useTimer(30)
  const mins = Math.floor(seconds / 60)
  const secs = seconds % 60

  return (
    <div>
      <h1 style={{ fontFamily: 'monospace', fontSize: '4rem' }}>
        {String(mins).padStart(2,'0')}:{String(secs).padStart(2,'0')}
      </h1>
      {!isRunning ? <button onClick={start}>Start</button>
                  : <button onClick={pause}>Pause</button>}
      <button onClick={reset}>Reset</button>
      {seconds === 0 && <p>⏳ Time is up!</p>}
    </div>
  )
}

Routing & Navigation

Basic Setup

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Router setup exists to connect URL paths to React components.

What if it did not exist?

Without routing, a single-page app cannot represent different screens with shareable URLs.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Wrap the app in a router and define routes for pages.

Intermediate

Use links for navigation instead of normal anchor reloads.

Advanced

Design route hierarchy before components become tangled.

Mastery

Make every important screen refreshable, bookmarkable, and meaningful from the URL alone.

Practice Ladder - Basic SetupGuided Practice

Create Home, Lessons, LessonDetail, and NotFound routes.

React Router maps URLs to components. A beginner goal is making links work without page reloads. A mastery goal is making every important screen refreshable, bookmarkable, and shareable.

Route Design Rules

  • Use normal path segments for real pages: /dashboard, /settings.
  • Use route params for identity: /courses/:courseId.
  • Use search params for filters and sorting: ?status=active&sort=recent.
  • Use nested routes when several screens share a layout.
jsx — main.jsx
import { BrowserRouter } from 'react-router-dom'

ReactDOM.createRoot(document.getElementById('root')).render(
  <BrowserRouter>
    <App />
  </BrowserRouter>
)
jsx — App.jsx
import { Routes, Route, Link, NavLink } from 'react-router-dom'
import Home from './pages/Home'
import About from './pages/About'
import Users from './pages/Users'
import UserDetail from './pages/UserDetail'
import NotFound from './pages/NotFound'

function App() {
  return (
    <div>
      <nav>
        {/* NavLink adds 'active' class automatically */}
        <NavLink to="/">Home</NavLink>
        <NavLink to="/about">About</NavLink>
        <NavLink to="/users">Users</NavLink>
      </nav>

      <Routes>
        <Route path="/" element={<Home />} />
        <Route path="/about" element={<About />} />
        <Route path="/users" element={<Users />} />
        {/* Dynamic route — :id is a URL parameter */}
        <Route path="/users/:id" element={<UserDetail />} />
        <Route path="*" element={<NotFound />} />
      </Routes>
    </div>
  )
}

Practice Ladder - URL Params & Navigation HooksGuided Practice

Build /lessons/:lessonId and show a friendly not-found state for unknown ids.

jsx — UserDetail.jsx
import { useParams, useNavigate } from 'react-router-dom'
import { useFetch } from '../hooks/useFetch'

function UserDetail() {
  const { id } = useParams()      // reads :id from URL
  const navigate = useNavigate()  // programmatic navigation
  const { data: user, loading } = useFetch(
    `https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/users/${id}`
  )

  if (loading) return <p>Loading...</p>

  return (
    <div>
      <h1>{user?.name}</h1>
      <p>{user?.email}</p>
      <button onClick={() => navigate(-1)}>← Back</button>
      <button onClick={() => navigate('/users')}>All Users</button>
    </div>
  )
}

// Search params: /search?q=react&page=1
import { useSearchParams } from 'react-router-dom'

function SearchPage() {
  const [searchParams, setSearchParams] = useSearchParams()
  const query = searchParams.get('q') || ''

  return (
    <input
      value={query}
      onChange={e => setSearchParams({ q: e.target.value })}
      placeholder="Search..."
    />
  )
}

Exercise 6.1 — Blog with Routes Day 6 · Exercise 1

Build a blog app with three pages: Home (list of post titles as links), Post (show post body using URL param :postId, fetched from JSONPlaceholder), and 404 page. Include a navigation bar that persists across all pages.

jsx — App.jsx
import { BrowserRouter, Routes, Route, Link, useParams } from 'react-router-dom'
import { useFetch } from './hooks/useFetch'

function Nav() {
  return <nav><Link to="/">Blog Home</Link></nav>
}

function Home() {
  const { data: posts, loading } = useFetch('https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts?_limit=10')
  if (loading) return <p>Loading...</p>
  return (
    <ul>
      {posts?.map(p => (
        <li key={p.id}><Link to={`/post/${p.id}`}>{p.title}</Link></li>
      ))}
    </ul>
  )
}

function Post() {
  const { postId } = useParams()
  const { data: post, loading } = useFetch(`https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts/${postId}`)
  if (loading) return <p>Loading...</p>
  return (<article><h1>{post?.title}</h1><p>{post?.body}</p></article>)
}

export default function App() {
  return (
    <BrowserRouter><Nav />
      <Routes>
        <Route path="/" element={<Home />} />
        <Route path="/post/:postId" element={<Post />} />
        <Route path="*" element={<h2>404 — Page Not Found</h2>} />
      </Routes>
    </BrowserRouter>
  )
}

CSS Modules

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

CSS Modules exist to make CSS class names local to a component by default.

What if it did not exist?

Without scoping, class names can collide and a style written for one component can unexpectedly affect another.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Import a CSS module and use styles.className.

Intermediate

Use modules for component-specific styling and CSS variables for shared tokens.

Advanced

Compose states and variants without turning class logic into a mess.

Mastery

Keep styles close to components while preserving system-wide visual consistency.

Practice Ladder - CSS ModulesGuided Practice

Style a LessonCard with default, active, and completed states using a CSS module.

CSS Modules are plain CSS with local class names. Beginners get safety from accidental global conflicts. Intermediate developers use modules to keep component styles close to components. Experts combine modules with design tokens so the app still feels consistent.

CSS Module Example

jsx
import styles from './LessonCard.module.css'

function LessonCard({ title, active }) {
  return (
    <article className={active ? styles.activeCard : styles.card}>
      <h3>{title}</h3>
    </article>
  )
}

CSS Modules scope styles to the component automatically — no class name collisions.

css — Button.module.css
.button {
  background: #3b82f6;
  color: white;
  padding: 0.5rem 1.25rem;
  border: none;
  border-radius: 6px;
  cursor: pointer;
  font-size: 1rem;
  transition: background 0.2s;
}

.button:hover { background: #2563eb; }
.button.secondary { background: #6b7280; }
.button.secondary:hover { background: #4b5563; }
jsx — Button.jsx
import styles from './Button.module.css'

function Button({ children, variant = 'primary' }) {
  return (
    <button className={`${styles.button} ${variant === 'secondary' ? styles.secondary : ''}`}>
      {children}
    </button>
  )
}

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Extract components when repeated utility strings become noisy. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
className={active ? styles.active : styles.card}

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Customize tokens and variants to match a real design system. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
use design tokens for color and spacing

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Use Tailwind as a constraint system, not as a license for inconsistent design. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
consistent system, flexible components

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

// A full card component using only Tailwind classes
function ProductCard({ name, price, image, badge }) {
  return (
    <div className="max-w-sm rounded-xl overflow-hidden shadow-lg bg-white hover:shadow-xl transition-shadow">
      <div className="relative">
        <img src={image} alt={name} className="w-full h-48 object-cover" />
        {badge && (
          <span className="absolute top-2 right-2 bg-red-500 text-white text-xs font-bold px-2 py-1 rounded-full">
            {badge}
          </span>
        )}
      </div>
      <div className="p-5">
        <h3 className="text-xl font-bold text-gray-900 mb-2">{name}</h3>
        <p className="text-2xl font-black text-blue-600">${price}</p>
        <button className="mt-4 w-full bg-blue-600 hover:bg-blue-700 text-white font-semibold py-2 px-4 rounded-lg transition-colors">
          Add to Cart
        </button>
      </div>
    </div>
  )
}

Conditional Tailwind with clsx

bash + jsx
npm install clsx

import clsx from 'clsx'

function Alert({ type, message }) {
  return (
    <div className={clsx(
      'p-4 rounded-lg border font-medium',
      {
        'bg-green-50 border-green-400 text-green-800': type === 'success',
        'bg-red-50 border-red-400 text-red-800': type === 'error',
        'bg-yellow-50 border-yellow-400 text-yellow-800': type === 'warning',
      }
    )}>
      {message}
    </div>
  )
}

const colors = [
  { name: 'Ocean Blue', hex: '#3b82f6' },
  { name: 'Forest Green', hex: '#22c55e' },
  { name: 'Sunset Red', hex: '#ef4444' },

]

function ColorGrid() {
  return (
    <div className="grid grid-cols-1 sm:grid-cols-2 lg:grid-cols-3 gap-4 p-6">
      {colors.map(c => (
        <div key={c.hex} className="rounded-xl overflow-hidden shadow-md">

}
Day 7 — Deep Dive

Animation with Framer Motion

Add professional animations to your React app with the most popular animation library.

bash
npm install framer-motion

Basic Animations

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Animations exist to communicate change: entering, leaving, expanding, reordering, or drawing attention.

What if it did not exist?

Without animation, some state changes can feel abrupt or disorienting.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Start with opacity and small movement.

Intermediate

Animate layout changes and list entry/exit carefully.

Advanced

Respect reduced-motion preferences and avoid blocking interaction.

Mastery

Use motion as feedback, not decoration.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Animations exist to communicate change: entering, leaving, expanding, reordering, or drawing attention. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Basic Animations, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
<motion.div initial={{ opacity: 0 }} animate={{ opacity: 1 }} />

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without animation, some state changes can feel abrupt or disorienting. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.

without this concept
// state changes can feel abrupt or confusing

Learning Ladder in Detail

Beginner Level - Build the First Mental Model

Start with opacity and small movement. At this stage, your job is not speed. Your job is to say what each line does in plain language. Use tiny inputs and tiny outputs. If you cannot predict the result before running it, make the example smaller.

beginner example
fade in a modal

Beginner practice: copy the example exactly, run it, then change one name and one value. Explain what changed and what stayed the same.

Intermediate Level - Use It in Normal React Work

Animate layout changes and list entry/exit carefully. At this level, combine the concept with a nearby React task: rendering a list, handling a click, updating state, reading route data, validating a form, or calling an API. The goal is to use the concept while building something recognizable.

intermediate example
animate item removal

Intermediate practice: rebuild the example in a component and add one realistic variation, such as an empty state, a disabled button, or a second data item.

Advanced Level - Handle Edge Cases and Tradeoffs

Respect reduced-motion preferences and avoid blocking interaction. Advanced understanding begins when you know where the concept can mislead you. Ask what happens with missing data, repeated renders, mutation, stale values, deeply nested state, slow networks, conflicts, accessibility, or team workflow.

advanced example
respect prefers-reduced-motion

Advanced practice: intentionally create a failure case, observe the bug, and then fix it using the concept correctly.

Mastery Level - Choose Deliberately

Use motion as feedback, not decoration. Mastery means you can explain not only how to use the concept, but when not to use it. You should be able to compare alternatives, keep the code readable for another developer, and connect the decision to maintainability.

mastery example
motion explains change, never blocks work

Mastery practice: teach the concept back in five sentences, refactor one messy example into a cleaner version, and write down the tradeoff you chose.

Practice Ladder - Basic AnimationsGuided Practice

Animate a modal opening, list item removal, and tab indicator movement.

Animation should explain a change: something entered, left, moved, expanded, or needs attention. Beginners can start with opacity and movement. Mastery means using motion sparingly and respecting users who prefer reduced motion.

Motion Purpose Checklist

  • Does the animation help the user understand what changed?
  • Is it short enough that repeated use still feels fast?
  • Does the interface still work clearly without animation?
  • Would this be distracting inside a focused work screen?
jsx
import { motion, AnimatePresence } from 'framer-motion'

// Replace any HTML element with motion.element
// initial: starting state | animate: end state | transition: how

function FadeIn({ children }) {
  return (
    <motion.div
      initial={{ opacity: 0, y: 20 }}
      animate={{ opacity: 1, y: 0 }}
      transition={{ duration: 0.4, ease: 'easeOut' }}
    >
      {children}
    </motion.div>
  )
}

// Hover and tap animations
function AnimatedButton({ children, onClick }) {
  return (
    <motion.button
      onClick={onClick}
      whileHover={{ scale: 1.05, backgroundColor: '#2563eb' }}
      whileTap={{ scale: 0.95 }}
      transition={{ type: 'spring', stiffness: 400, damping: 17 }}
    >
      {children}
    </motion.button>
  )
}

// AnimatePresence — animate when components mount/unmount
function ToastNotification({ message, visible }) {
  return (
    <AnimatePresence>
      {visible && (
        <motion.div
          initial={{ opacity: 0, x: 100 }}
          animate={{ opacity: 1, x: 0 }}
          exit={{ opacity: 0, x: 100 }}
          transition={{ type: 'spring', stiffness: 300, damping: 25 }}
          style={{ position: 'fixed', bottom: 24, right: 24,
                   background: '#1a1209', color: 'white', padding: '12px 20px',
                   borderRadius: 8 }}
        >
          {message}
        </motion.div>
      )}
    </AnimatePresence>
  )
}

Stagger Children Animations

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

Staggering exists to sequence multiple child animations so groups feel organized instead of chaotic.

What if it did not exist?

Without stagger, many items can animate at once and overwhelm the user.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Delay each child slightly.

Intermediate

Use stagger for menus, cards, and onboarding steps.

Advanced

Keep total duration short so the UI remains fast.

Mastery

Use sequencing only where it improves comprehension.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

Staggering exists to sequence multiple child animations so groups feel organized instead of chaotic. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

example

Practice Ladder - Stagger Children AnimationsGuided Practice

Animate five lesson cards entering with a short stagger and compare it to all-at-once animation.

jsx
const containerVariants = {
  hidden: {},
  show: {
    transition: {
      staggerChildren: 0.1  // 100ms between each child
    }
  }
}

const itemVariants = {
  hidden: { opacity: 0, y: 20 },
  show:   { opacity: 1, y: 0, transition: { type: 'spring', stiffness: 300 } }
}

function StaggeredList({ items }) {
  return (
    <motion.ul variants={containerVariants} initial="hidden" animate="show">
      {items.map(item => (
        <motion.li key={item.id} variants={itemVariants}>
          {item.name}
        </motion.li>
      ))}
    </motion.ul>
  )
}

// Page transition wrapper
const pageTransition = {
  initial:  { opacity: 0, x: -20 },
  animate:  { opacity: 1, x: 0 },
  exit:     { opacity: 0, x: 20 },
  transition: { duration: 0.25 }
}

function PageWrapper({ children }) {
  return <motion.div {...pageTransition}>{children}</motion.div>
}
Exercise 7B.1 — Animated Card GridDay 7B · Exercise

Build a product card grid where: (a) cards fade + slide in with stagger on mount, (b) each card scales up on hover, (c) clicking a card shows a modal that animates in from the bottom (use AnimatePresence), (d) the modal can be closed with a button or Escape key.

jsx
import { useState, useEffect } from 'react'
import { motion, AnimatePresence } from 'framer-motion'

const PRODUCTS = [
  { id: 1, name: 'Laptop',     price: 999, emoji: '💻' },
  { id: 2, name: 'Headphones', price: 79,  emoji: '🎧' },
  { id: 3, name: 'Camera',     price: 549, emoji: '📷' },
]

const list  = { hidden: {}, show: { transition: { staggerChildren: 0.12 } } }
const item  = { hidden: { opacity: 0, y: 30 }, show: { opacity: 1, y: 0 } }

export default function AnimatedGrid() {
  const [selected, setSelected] = useState(null)

  useEffect(() => {
    const onKey = e => { if (e.key === 'Escape') setSelected(null) }
    window.addEventListener('keydown', onKey)
    return () => window.removeEventListener('keydown', onKey)
  }, [])

  return (
    <>
      <motion.div
        variants={list} initial="hidden" animate="show"
        style={{ display: 'grid', gridTemplateColumns: 'repeat(3,1fr)', gap: 16 }}
      >
        {PRODUCTS.map(p => (
          <motion.div
            key={p.id} variants={item}
            whileHover={{ scale: 1.04, boxShadow: '0 8px 24px rgba(0,0,0,.15)' }}
            whileTap={{ scale: 0.98 }}
            onClick={() => setSelected(p)}
            style={{ padding: 24, background: 'white', borderRadius: 12,
                     cursor: 'pointer', textAlign: 'center', border: '1px solid #eee' }}
          >
            <div style={{ fontSize: '3rem' }}>{p.emoji}</div>
            <h3>{p.name}</h3>
            <p>${p.price}</p>
          </motion.div>
        ))}
      </motion.div>

      <AnimatePresence>
        {selected && (
          <motion.div
            style={{ position:'fixed',inset:0,background:'rgba(0,0,0,.5)',
                     display:'flex',alignItems:'flex-end',justifyContent:'center' }}
            initial={{ opacity: 0 }} animate={{ opacity: 1 }} exit={{ opacity: 0 }}
            onClick={() => setSelected(null)}
          >
            <motion.div
              initial={{ y: '100%' }} animate={{ y: 0 }} exit={{ y: '100%' }}
              transition={{ type: 'spring', damping: 30, stiffness: 300 }}
              style={{ background: 'white', padding: 32, borderRadius: '16px 16px 0 0',
                       width: '100%', maxWidth: 480 }}
              onClick={e => e.stopPropagation()}
            >
              <div style={{ fontSize: '4rem', textAlign: 'center' }}>{selected.emoji}</div>
              <h2>{selected.name}</h2>
              <p>Price: ${selected.price}</p>
              <button onClick={() => setSelected(null)}>Close</button>
            </motion.div>
          </motion.div>
        )}
      </AnimatePresence>
    </>
  )
}
Day 8

State Management — Zustand

Global state management the simple, scalable way.

💡 When to use global state?
Use global state when the same data is needed by many components at different levels of the tree, and prop drilling becomes painful. For co-located state, useState is still best.
bash
npm install zustand

Creating a Store

Purpose, From First Principles

Why it exists

A store exists to share client state across unrelated components without prop drilling.

What if it did not exist?

Without a store, app-wide state may be duplicated, lifted too high, or passed through many components.

Learning Ladder

Beginner

Use a store for state that many distant components need.

Intermediate

Keep actions near the state they change.

Advanced

Split stores or selectors to avoid unnecessary re-renders.

Mastery

Choose local, URL, server, context, or global state based on ownership, not habit.

Why It Exists - Detailed Explanation

A store exists to share client state across unrelated components without prop drilling. In a real project, this concept gives a name and a repeatable pattern to a problem you will meet many times. Beginners should first connect the concept to a concrete pain: repeated code, unsafe changes, unclear data flow, hard-to-share screens, or unreliable releases. Once that pain is visible, the feature stops feeling like syntax and starts feeling like a tool.

Example: when working with Creating a Store, look for the smallest situation where the idea removes confusion or prevents a bug.

example
const useStore = create(set => ({ count: 0, inc: () => set(s => ({ count: s.count + 1 })) }))

What If It Did Not Exist? - Detailed Explanation

Without a store, app-wide state may be duplicated, lifted too high, or passed through many components. This does not always mean the app is impossible to build. It usually means the developer must solve the same problem manually every time. Manual solutions are longer, easier to forget, harder to review, and more likely to behave differently across files.

The beginner lesson is important: when you see the older or manual style below, do not only think "this is wrong." Think "this is the pressure that caused the better tool or pattern to exist." That is how solid understanding forms.
